<div id="review_27631795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27631795" />
    <a title="Leah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/855350-leah"><img alt="Leah" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27631795?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah" href="/user/show/855350-leah">Leah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27631795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16449615028577381215">Finally finished this book. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_With_The_Wind' title='Gone With The Wind by Margaret Mitchell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gone With the Wind</a> is my favorite classic, so I am always eager to read the authorized follow-ups. Um...yeah. So far two strike outs (don't even get me started on Alexandra Ripley's sequal!). I feel like I could have really appreciated the complexities of some of McCaig's supporting characters if only his prose wasn't so choppy, awkward, and bland. Where was the passion? The grandeur? The glorious description of the South pre Civil War and the bleakness following the </span>
  <span id="freeText16449615028577381215" style="display:none">Finally finished this book. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_With_The_Wind" title="Gone With The Wind by Margaret Mitchell" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone With the Wind</a> is my favorite classic, so I am always eager to read the authorized follow-ups. Um...yeah. So far two strike outs (don't even get me started on Alexandra Ripley's sequal!). I feel like I could have really appreciated the complexities of some of McCaig's supporting characters if only his prose wasn't so choppy, awkward, and bland. Where was the passion? The grandeur? The glorious description of the South pre Civil War and the bleakness following the surrender? Scarlett in this book was so two dimensional! And Rhett's finest moments came in the very beginning as you witnessed his rough path to adulthood. The best characters in my opinion were Rosemary Butler and Andrew Ravenal and even then I only mildly tolerated the first and ended up loathing the second (although I guess the fact that I was able to feel such a strong emotion has to give the author some credit). <br /><br />Okay, to be fair, there are a few things I didn't mind so much (hence the second star). 1) The ending. Much more believable than Ripley's sequel. Thank god no one travelled to Ireland. 2) Melanie was explored more (through her letters to Rosemary) which allowed me to see how incredibly perceptive she truly was. 3) McCaig did a really good job describing two horrific scenes--that of the Trunk Master's execution and what ends up happening to Tunis Bonneau. Both scenes literally make me cringe. <br /><br />I hope the next author who tackles GWTW (if there is another attempt) does a better job. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16449615028577381215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Linda Hart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart"><img alt="Linda Hart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549256566p2/2455252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Hart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Hart" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart">Linda Hart</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330455824" href="/book/show/3726925-rhett-butlers-people">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1241383137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14802598691069313087">I enjoyed <u>Rhett Butler's People</u> so much that I was disappointed when it ended. The author was commissioned by the estate of Margaret Mitchell to write this "parallel sequel" to <u>Gone With The Wind</u>, and he has kept faithful to her style and to the story and details of Mitchell's original classic. This gives an account of Rhett's life prior to meeting Scarlett and describes the lives of his friends and family. In doing so, it gives a well-researched look into life in Charleston Atlanta just prior t</span>
  <span id="freeText14802598691069313087" style="display:none">I enjoyed <u>Rhett Butler's People</u> so much that I was disappointed when it ended. The author was commissioned by the estate of Margaret Mitchell to write this "parallel sequel" to <u>Gone With The Wind</u>, and he has kept faithful to her style and to the story and details of Mitchell's original classic. This gives an account of Rhett's life prior to meeting Scarlett and describes the lives of his friends and family. In doing so, it gives a well-researched look into life in Charleston Atlanta just prior to and during the civil war. <br /><br />Certainly, there are a million possibilities for his story, but I enjoyed this take. I really enjoyed <u>Gone With the Wind</u>, but Rhett was my favorite character and there were so many unanswered questions about him. They are answered in this fleshed out sequel/addition, which helps the reader to understand and appreciate his character all the more. <br /><br />I think the author did a wonderful job of tying the stories together and maintaining the characters and setting. He had to satisfy two audiences - Margaret Mitchell fans and Civil War buffs. That he was able to offer such a satisfying read, while tying in small tidbits from the original story (e.g. how Scarlett came to be in possession of the yellow scarf she gave to Ashley is particularly clever), is impressive indeed. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14802598691069313087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5836649966694875648">I gave a similar review to "Scarlett" the sequel by Alexandra Ripley. I read this book because I (like most GWTW fans) will always thirst for more of this story even as I know that I'll never really get it. Only Mitchell can write this story, and any other attempt will always fall short. <br/><br/>The Rhett in this book is not the calculating, capitalizing scoundrel of Mitchell's story. McCaig's Rhett is written as a misunderstood hero. This is a different man than the one I've come to understand through </span>
  <span id="freeText5836649966694875648" style="display:none">I gave a similar review to "Scarlett" the sequel by Alexandra Ripley. I read this book because I (like most GWTW fans) will always thirst for more of this story even as I know that I'll never really get it. Only Mitchell can write this story, and any other attempt will always fall short. <br /><br />The Rhett in this book is not the calculating, capitalizing scoundrel of Mitchell's story. McCaig's Rhett is written as a misunderstood hero. This is a different man than the one I've come to understand through Mitchell's writing, and I don't like him nearly as much. I'd rather believe that Rhett was really truly a rake at heart and only one woman (who was a kindred spirit) could capture his heart - versus believing McCaig's version that makes Rhett seem like he was never really a rake to begin with. <br /><br />One thing I found interesting was that the ending to this book completely discounts the entire "Scarlett" sequel. In other words, the way this book ends the story between Rhett and Scarlett would make it impossible for the sequel to have occurred at all. How much more clear could it be that we all have our unique versions of how their story ended? <br /><br />To me this is one writer's opinion of what may have happened behind the scenes, but it will never be part of the real story. It is a stand alone book, just like "Scarlett" was. Regardless, I still enjoyed reading it because it fed my addiction to a story that will never have an ending. For that, it was still worth reading for me. I will most likely read anything related to the GWTW story that I can get my hands on - even knowing that it will never quite measure up. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5836649966694875648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1138220161" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2539006620161687541">I skimmed a few of the reviews prior to beginning this story and found many pro and many con. As a lifelong devotee of Ms. Mitchell's 'Gone With The Wind', and an acquaintance of the followup 'Scarlett', I was equally nervous and excited to read this account of Rhett Butler's life and backstory according to Mr. McCaig. I dove in with an open mind, keeping the notion forefront that - while nothing can compare to the original work and original creation of the characters - perhaps Mr. McCaig's idea</span>
  <span id="freeText2539006620161687541" style="display:none">I skimmed a few of the reviews prior to beginning this story and found many pro and many con. As a lifelong devotee of Ms. Mitchell's 'Gone With The Wind', and an acquaintance of the followup 'Scarlett', I was equally nervous and excited to read this account of Rhett Butler's life and backstory according to Mr. McCaig. I dove in with an open mind, keeping the notion forefront that - while nothing can compare to the original work and original creation of the characters - perhaps Mr. McCaig's idea of Rhett Butler's life before and during his time with Miss Scarlet O'Hara wouldn't be as bad as some people have opinionated it to be. And I can say that I am pleasantly surprised by the outcome. I found myself drawn into the pages, unable to put it down. The story flowed smoothly and in time with what I know from the original and I appreciated the ideas of what happened in Rhett's life to make him who he was. Is this GWTW? No. Is it "as good as" GWTW? I would say no. Is it worth the time to read if you want to learn more of Mr. Butler and his heritage, family, and life events? Absolutly! If you're a lover of the original southern tale as well as the sequel, I recommend reading this as well as another supplement to who or what the character of Rhett Butler is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2539006620161687541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_13258700" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13258700" />
    <a title="Lauren Denton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/418235-lauren-denton"><img alt="Lauren Denton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464900968p2/418235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13258700?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren Denton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren Denton" href="/user/show/418235-lauren-denton">Lauren Denton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312262518" href="/book/show/1079914.Rhett_Butler_s_People">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13258700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9621726673707572552">This book doesn't so much retell <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_With_The_Wind' title='Gone With The Wind by Margaret Mitchell' rel='nofollow noopener'>Gone with the Wind</a> from Rhett's view (like I originally thought) as it does give the account of Rhett's life prior to meeting Scarlett and describes the lives of his friends and family. In doing so, it gives a well-researched look into life in Charleston Atlanta just prior to and during the cival war. <br/><br/>I enjoyed it mostly for the historical aspects--it gave a different view of the cival war period than GWTW did. McCaig is great in describing individual battles, re</span>
  <span id="freeText9621726673707572552" style="display:none">This book doesn't so much retell <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18405.Gone_With_The_Wind" title="Gone With The Wind by Margaret Mitchell" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone with the Wind</a> from Rhett's view (like I originally thought) as it does give the account of Rhett's life prior to meeting Scarlett and describes the lives of his friends and family. In doing so, it gives a well-researched look into life in Charleston Atlanta just prior to and during the cival war. <br /><br />I enjoyed it mostly for the historical aspects--it gave a different view of the cival war period than GWTW did. McCaig is great in describing individual battles, relations between blacks and whites, and the destruction of Southern cities by the "Federals," but in my opinion, wasn't so hot at the dialogue--mainly dialogue between men and women. It seemed either forced or cheesy to me. I gave it 4 stars because I enjoyed it more than 3 stars worth, but if given the choice, I'd probably make it 3.5 stars. Worth reading for the history lessons though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9621726673707572552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UvbWUgZIeX+IN8HeB7pkqjjOpY5uTL8J/LwqzNzSiAkUcYkVVRrL6Vkhfj4QLhCRbtrt6E8qD8W8Pv/HPy4TPQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mmKZ2qE3pTCDEIKT6pnvbNTgEErffLYv+AmQ8rTzTwPc5cad8mUXplIGPXP9DZtXgvRYLP4aBuO4i0X5Vw/UNw==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_546018'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/546018.Roots?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Roots: The Saga of an American Family<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/17434.Alex_Haley?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Alex Haley<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.43 avg rating &mdash; 147,223 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1976\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer15084188107073208670\">When he was a boy in Henning, Tennessee, Alex Haley\'s grandmother used to tell him stories about their family—stories that went back to her grandparents, and their grandparents, down through the generations all the way to a man she called \"the Africa<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15084188107073208670\" style=\"display:none\">When he was a boy in Henning, Tennessee, Alex Haley\'s grandmother used to tell him stories about their family—stories that went back to her grandparents, and their grandparents, down through the generations all the way to a man she called &quot;the African.&quot; She said he had lived across the ocean near what he called the &quot;Kamby Bolongo&quot; and had been out in the forest one day chopping wood to make a drum when he was set upon by four men, beaten, chained and dragged aboard a slave ship bound for Colonial America.\n\nStill vividly remembering the stories after he grew up and became a writer, Haley began to search for documentation that might authenticate the narrative. It took ten years and a half a million miles of travel across three continents to find it, but finally, in an astonishing feat of genealogical detective work, he discovered not only the name of &quot;the African&quot;—Kunta Kinte—but the precise location of Juffure, the very village in The Gambia, West Africa, from which he was abducted in 1767 at the age of sixteen and taken on the Lord Ligonier to Maryland and sold to a Virginia planter.\n\nHaley has talked in Juffure with his own African sixth cousins. On September 29, 1967, he stood on the dock in Annapolis where his great-great-great-great-grandfather was taken ashore on September 29, 1767. Now he has written the monumental two-century drama of Kunta Kinte and the six generations who came after him—slaves and freedmen, farmers and blacksmiths, lumber mill workers and Pullman porters, lawyers and architects—and one author.\n\nBut Haley has done more than recapture the history of his own family. As the first black American writer to trace his origins back to their roots, he has told the story of 25,000,000 Americans of African descent. He has rediscovered for an entire people a rich cultural heritage that slavery took away from them, along with their names and their identities. But Roots speaks, finally, not just to blacks, or to whites, but to all people and all races everywhere, for the story it tells is one of the most eloquent testimonials ever written to the indomitability of the human spirit.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15084188107073208670\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13276935576163949222"><strong>Donald McCaig</strong> was the award-winning author of <em>Jacob’s Ladder,</em> designated “the best civil war novel ever written” by <em>The Virginia Quarterly</em>. <em>People</em> magazine raved “Think <em>Gone With the Wind</em>, think <em>Cold Mountain</em>.” It won the Michael Sharra Award for Civil War Fiction and the Library of Virginia Award for Fiction. <br/><br/>Donald McCaig wrote about rural American life, sheepdogs, and the Civil War. He also wro</span>
  <span id="freeText13276935576163949222" style="display:none"><strong>Donald McCaig</strong> was the award-winning author of <em>Jacob’s Ladder,</em> designated “the best civil war novel ever written” by <em>The Virginia Quarterly</em>. <em>People</em> magazine raved “Think <em>Gone With the Wind</em>, think <em>Cold Mountain</em>.” It won the Michael Sharra Award for Civil War Fiction and the Library of Virginia Award for Fiction. <br /><br />Donald McCaig wrote about rural American life, sheepdogs, and the Civil War. He also wrote poetry and wrote under various pseudonyms.</span>
